See respective team articles for more information.</i>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_baseball_in_the_United_States" title="History of baseball in the United States">History of baseball in the United States</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Founding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the 1860s, aided by soldiers playing the game in camp during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, "New York"-style baseball expanded into a national game and spawned baseball\'s first governing body, <a href="/wiki/History_of_baseball_in_the_United_States" title="History of baseball in the United States">The National Association of Base Ball Players</a>. The NABBP existed as an amateur league for 12 years. By 1867, more than 400 clubs were members. Most of the strongest clubs remained those based in the northeastern U.S. For <a href="/wiki/Professional_baseball" title="Professional baseball">professional baseball</a>\'s founding year, MLB uses the year 1869\xe2\x80\x94when the first professional team, the <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Red_Stockings" title="Cincinnati Red Stockings">Cincinnati Red Stockings</a>, was established.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A schism developed between professional and amateur ballplayers after the founding of the Cincinnati club. The NABBP split into an amateur organization and a professional organization. The <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Professional_Base_Ball_Players" title="National Association of Professional Base Ball Players">National Association of Professional Base Ball Players</a>, often known as the National Association (NA), was formed in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-base_ball_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-base_ball-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Its amateur counterpart disappeared after only a few years.<sup id="cite_ref-Rader_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rader-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The modern <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Braves" title="Atlanta Braves">Atlanta Braves</a> franchises trace their histories back to the National Association of Professional Base Ball Players in the 1870s.<sup id="cite_ref-Spatz_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spatz-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1876, the National League of Professional Base Ball Clubs (later known as the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> or NL) was established after the NA proved ineffective. The league placed its emphasis on clubs rather than on players. Clubs could now enforce player contracts, preventing players from jumping to higher-paying clubs. Clubs were required to play the full schedule of games instead of forfeiting scheduled games when the club was no longer in the running for the league championship, which happened frequently under the NA. A concerted effort was made to curb gambling on games, which was leaving the validity of results in doubt. The first game in the NL\xe2\x80\x94on Saturday, April 22, 1876 (at the <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Street_Grounds" title="Jefferson Street Grounds">Jefferson Street Grounds</a>, Philadelphia)\xe2\x80\x94is often pointed to as the beginning of MLB.<sup id="cite_ref-noble_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noble-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1896_Baltimore_Orioles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/1896_Baltimore_Orioles.jpg/220px-1896_Baltimore_Orioles.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/1896_Baltimore_Orioles.jpg/330px-1896_Baltimore_Orioles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/1896_Baltimore_Orioles.jpg/440px-1896_Baltimore_Orioles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="884" data-file-height="643" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1896_Baltimore_Orioles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>National League <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles_(19th_century)" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltimore Orioles (19th century)">Baltimore Orioles</a>, 1896</div></div></div>\n<p>The early years of the NL were tumultuous, with threats from rival leagues and a rebellion by players against the hated "reserve clause", which restricted the free movement of players between clubs. Competitor leagues formed regularly and also disbanded regularly. The most successful was the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_(19th_century)" title="American Association (19th century)">American Association</a> (1882\xe2\x80\x931891), sometimes called the "beer and whiskey league" for its tolerance of the sale of alcoholic beverages to spectators. For several years, the NL and American Association champions met in a postseason championship series\xe2\x80\x94the first attempt at a <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a>. The two leagues merged in 1892 as a single 12-team NL, but the NL dropped four teams after the 1899 season. This led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> in 1901 under AL president <a href="/wiki/Ban_Johnson" title="Ban Johnson">Ban Johnson</a>, and the resulting bidding war for players led to widespread contract-breaking and legal disputes.\n</p><p>The war between the AL and NL caused shock waves throughout the baseball world. At a meeting at the Leland Hotel in Chicago in 1901, the other baseball leagues negotiated a plan to maintain their independence. A new National Association was formed to oversee these minor leagues.<sup id="cite_ref-hotel_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hotel-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> While the NA continues to this day (known as <a href="/wiki/Minor_League_Baseball" title="Minor League Baseball">Minor League Baseball</a>), at the time Ban Johnson saw it as a tool to end threats from smaller rivals who might expand in other territories and threaten his league\'s dominance.\n</p><p>After 1902, the NL, AL, and NA signed a new National Agreement which tied independent contracts to the reserve-clause contracts. The agreement also set up a formal classification system for minor leagues, the forerunner of today\'s system that was refined by <a href="/wiki/Branch_Rickey" title="Branch Rickey">Branch Rickey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nabpl_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nabpl-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several other early defunct baseball leagues are officially considered major leagues, and their statistics and records are included with those of the two current major leagues. These include the AA, the <a href="/wiki/Union_Association" title="Union Association">Union Association</a> (1884),<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Players%27_League" title="Players&#39; League">Players\' League</a> (1890),<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_League" title="Federal League">Federal League</a> (1914\xe2\x80\x931915).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Both the UA and AA are considered major leagues by many baseball researchers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> because of the perceived high caliber of play and the number of star players featured. Some researchers, including <a href="/wiki/Nate_Silver" title="Nate Silver">Nate Silver</a>, dispute the major-league status of the UA by pointing out that franchises came and went and that the St. Louis club was deliberately "stacked"; the St. Louis club was owned by the league\'s president and it was the only club that was close to major-league caliber.<sup id="cite_ref-union_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-union-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dead-ball_era">Dead-ball era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Dead-ball era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dead-ball_era" title="Dead-ball era">Dead-ball era</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:T205_Cy_Young.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/T205_Cy_Young.jpg/170px-T205_Cy_Young.jpg" width="170" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/T205_Cy_Young.jpg/255px-T205_Cy_Young.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/T205_Cy_Young.jpg/340px-T205_Cy_Young.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1674" data-file-height="2964" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:T205_Cy_Young.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cy Young, 1911 baseball card</div></div></div>\n<p>The period between 1900 and 1919 is commonly referred to as the "Dead-Ball Era." Games of this era tended to be low scoring and were often dominated by pitchers, such as <a href="/wiki/Walter_Johnson" title="Walter Johnson">Walter Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young" title="Cy Young">Cy Young</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christy_Mathewson" title="Christy Mathewson">Christy Mathewson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mordecai_Brown" title="Mordecai Brown">Mordecai Brown</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland_Alexander" title="Grover Cleveland Alexander">Grover Cleveland Alexander</a>. The term also accurately describes the condition of the baseball itself. The baseball used American rather than the modern Australian wool yarn and was not wound as tightly, affecting the distance that it would travel.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> More significantly, balls were kept in play until they were mangled, soft and sometimes lopsided. During this era, a baseball cost three dollars, equal to $42.35 today (in <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a>-adjusted <a href="/wiki/U.S._dollars" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. dollars">U.S. dollars</a>), and owners were reluctant to purchase new balls. Fans were expected to throw back fouls and (rare) home runs. Baseballs also became stained with tobacco juice, grass, and mud, and sometimes the juice of licorice, which some players would chew for the purpose of discoloring the ball.<sup id="cite_ref-Keating_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keating-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Also, pitchers could manipulate the ball through the use of the <a href="/wiki/Spitball" title="Spitball">spitball</a>. (In 1921 use of this pitch was restricted to a few pitchers with a <a href="/wiki/Grandfather_clause" title="Grandfather clause">grandfather clause</a>). Additionally, many ballparks had large dimensions, such as the <a href="/wiki/West_Side_Grounds" class="mw-redirect" title="West Side Grounds">West Side Grounds</a> of the Chicago Cubs, which was 560 feet (170&#160;m) to the center field fence, and the <a href="/wiki/Huntington_Avenue_Grounds" title="Huntington Avenue Grounds">Huntington Avenue Grounds</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>, which was 635 feet (194&#160;m) to the center field fence, thus home runs were rare, and "small ball" tactics such as <a href="/wiki/Single_(baseball)" title="Single (baseball)">singles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bunt_(baseball)" title="Bunt (baseball)">bunts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stolen_base" title="Stolen base">stolen bases</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Hit_and_run_(baseball)" title="Hit and run (baseball)">hit-and-run</a> play dominated the strategies of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Hitting methods like the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Chop" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltimore Chop">Baltimore Chop</a> were used to increase the number of infield singles.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> On a successful Baltimore chop, the batter hits the ball forcefully into the ground, causing it to bounce so high that the batter reaches first base before the ball can be fielded and thrown to the first baseman.<sup id="cite_ref-Lieb_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lieb-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The adoption of the foul strike rule in the early twentieth century quickly sent baseball from a high-scoring game to one where scoring runs became a struggle. Prior to the institution of this rule, foul balls were not counted as strikes: a batter could foul off any number of pitches with no strikes counted against him; this gave an enormous advantage to the batter. In 1901, the NL adopted the foul strike rule, and the AL followed suit in 1903.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/1919_World_Series" title="1919 World Series">1919 World Series</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a> and Cincinnati Reds, baseball was rocked by allegations of a <a href="/wiki/Game_fixing" class="mw-redirect" title="Game fixing">game fixing</a> scheme known as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sox_Scandal" title="Black Sox Scandal">Black Sox Scandal</a>. Eight players\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Shoeless_Joe_Jackson" title="Shoeless Joe Jackson">Joe Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Cicotte" title="Eddie Cicotte">Eddie Cicotte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lefty_Williams" title="Lefty Williams">Claude "Lefty" Williams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buck_Weaver" title="Buck Weaver">George "Buck" Weaver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chick_Gandil" title="Chick Gandil">Arnold "Chick" Gandil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fred_McMullin" title="Fred McMullin">Fred McMullin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swede_Risberg" title="Swede Risberg">Charles "Swede" Risberg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Happy_Felsch" title="Happy Felsch">Oscar "Happy" Felsch</a>\xe2\x80\x94intentionally lost the World Series in exchange for a ring worth $100,000 ($1,064,705.88 in 2016 dollars).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Despite being acquitted, all were permanently banned from Major League Baseball.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_in_popularity">Rise in popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Rise in popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Baseball\'s popularity increased in the 1920s and 1930s. The 1920 season was notable for the death of <a href="/wiki/Ray_Chapman" title="Ray Chapman">Ray Chapman</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Indians" title="Cleveland Indians">Cleveland Indians</a>. Chapman, who was struck in the head by a pitch and died a few hours later, became the only MLB player to die of an on-field injury, a tragedy which led directly to both leagues requiring the placing into play new, white baseballs whenever a ball became scuffed or dirty, helping bring the "dead-ball" era to an end. The following year, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a> made their first World Series appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Plaque_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plaque-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the 1930s, the team had appeared in 11 World Series, winning eight of them.<sup id="cite_ref-BR_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BR-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Yankees slugger <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> had set the single season home run record in 1927, hitting 60 home runs; a few years earlier, Ruth had set the same record with 29 home runs.<sup id="cite_ref-Swat_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swat-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Affected by the difficulties of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, baseball\'s popularity had begun a downward turn in the early 1930s. By 1932, only two MLB teams turned a profit. Attendance had fallen, due at least in part to a 10% federal amusement tax added to baseball ticket prices. Baseball owners cut their <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_rosters" title="Major League Baseball rosters">rosters</a> from 25 men to 23 men, and even the best players took pay cuts. Team executives were innovative in their attempts to survive, creating night games, broadcasting games live by radio and rolling out promotions such as free admission for women. Throughout the period of the Great Depression, no MLB teams moved or folded.<sup id="cite_ref-Belson_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belson-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_era">World War II era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: World War II era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The onset of World War II created a significant shortage of professional baseball players, as more than 500 men left MLB teams to serve in the military. Many of them played on service baseball teams that entertained military personnel in the US or in the Pacific. MLB teams of this time largely consisted of young men, older players, and those with a military classification of <a href="/wiki/4F_(military_conscription)" class="mw-redirect" title="4F (military conscription)">4F</a>, indicating mental, physical, or moral unsuitability for service. Men like <a href="/wiki/Pete_Gray" title="Pete Gray">Pete Gray</a>, a one-armed outfielder, got the chance to advance to the major leagues. However, MLB rosters did not include any black players through the end of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-War_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-War-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Black players, many of whom served in the war, were still restricted to playing <a href="/wiki/Negro_league_baseball" title="Negro league baseball">Negro league baseball</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Blackout_(wartime)" title="Blackout (wartime)">Wartime blackout restrictions</a>, designed to keep outdoor lighting at low levels, caused another problem for baseball. These rules limited traveling and night games to the point that the 1942 season nearly had to be canceled.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> On January 14, 1942, MLB Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis" title="Kenesaw Mountain Landis">Kenesaw Mountain Landis</a> wrote a letter to U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> and pleaded for the continuation of baseball during the war in hopes for a start of a new major league season. President Roosevelt responded, "I honestly feel that it would be best for the country to keep baseball going. There will be fewer people unemployed and everybody will work longer hours and harder than ever before. And that means that they ought to have a chance for recreation and for taking their minds off their work even more than before."<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the approval of President Roosevelt, spring training began in 1942 with few repercussions. The war interrupted the careers of stars including <a href="/wiki/Stan_Musial" title="Stan Musial">Stan Musial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Feller" title="Bob Feller">Bob Feller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ted_Williams" title="Ted Williams">Ted Williams</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a>, but baseball clubs continued to field their teams.<sup id="cite_ref-Weintraub_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weintraub-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breaking_the_color_barrier">Breaking the color barrier</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Breaking the color barrier">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baseball_color_line" title="Baseball color line">Baseball color line</a></div>\n<p>Branch Rickey, president and general manager of the Brooklyn Dodgers, began making efforts to introduce a black baseball player to the previously all-white professional baseball leagues in the mid-1940s. He selected <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">Jackie Robinson</a> from a list of promising Negro league players. After obtaining a commitment from Robinson to "turn the other cheek" to any racial antagonism directed at him, Rickey agreed to sign him to a contract for $600 a month. In what was later referred to as "The Noble Experiment", Robinson was the first black baseball player in the International League since the 1880s, joining the Dodgers\' farm club, the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Royals" title="Montreal Royals">Montreal Royals</a>, for the 1946 season.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The following year, the Dodgers called up Robinson to the major leagues. On April 15, 1947, Robinson made his major league debut at Ebbets Field before a crowd of 26,623 spectators, including more than 14,000 black patrons. Black baseball fans began flocking to see the Dodgers when they came to town, abandoning the Negro league teams that they had followed exclusively. Robinson\'s promotion met a generally positive, although mixed, reception among newspaper writers and white major league players. Manager <a href="/wiki/Leo_Durocher" title="Leo Durocher">Leo Durocher</a> informed his team, "I do not care if the guy is yellow or black, or if he has stripes like a fuckin\' zebra. I\'m the manager of this team, and I say he plays. What\'s more, I say he can make us all rich. And if any of you cannot use the money, I will see that you are all traded."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a strike threat by some players, NL President <a href="/wiki/Ford_Frick" title="Ford Frick">Ford Frick</a> and Baseball Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Happy_Chandler" title="Happy Chandler">Happy Chandler</a> let it be known that any striking players would be suspended. Robinson received significant encouragement from several major league players, including Dodgers teammate <a href="/wiki/Pee_Wee_Reese" title="Pee Wee Reese">Pee Wee Reese</a> who said, "You can hate a man for many reasons. Color is not one of them."<sup id="cite_ref-Newman_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> That year, Robinson won the inaugural <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Rookie_of_the_Year_Award" title="Major League Baseball Rookie of the Year Award">Major League Baseball Rookie of the Year Award</a> (separate NL and AL Rookie of the Year honors were not awarded until 1949).<sup id="cite_ref-ROY_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ROY-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Less than three months later, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Doby" title="Larry Doby">Larry Doby</a> became the first African-American to break the color barrier in the American League with the Cleveland Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-Doby_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doby-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The next year, a number of other black players entered the major leagues. <a href="/wiki/Satchel_Paige" title="Satchel Paige">Satchel Paige</a> was signed by the Indians and the Dodgers added star catcher <a href="/wiki/Roy_Campanella" title="Roy Campanella">Roy Campanella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Don_Newcombe" title="Don Newcombe">Don Newcombe</a>, who was later the first winner of the <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">Cy Young Award</a> for his outstanding pitching.<sup id="cite_ref-Finkelman_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Finkelman-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_in_baseball">Women in baseball</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Women in baseball">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_baseball" title="Women in baseball">Women in baseball</a></div>\n<p>MLB banned the signing of women to contracts in 1952, but that ban was lifted in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> As of 2018, there have been no female MLB players.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Expanding_west.2C_south_and_north"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Expanding_west,_south_and_north">Expanding west, south and north</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Expanding west, south and north">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_relocation_of_1950s%E2%80%931960s" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball relocation of 1950s\xe2\x80\x931960s">Major League Baseball relocation of 1950s\xe2\x80\x931960s</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dodger_Stadium.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Dodger_Stadium.jpg/220px-Dodger_Stadium.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Dodger_Stadium.jpg/330px-Dodger_Stadium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Dodger_Stadium.jpg/440px-Dodger_Stadium.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dodger_Stadium.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dodger_Stadium" title="Dodger Stadium">Dodger Stadium</a> in 2007</div></div></div>\n<p>From 1903 to 1953, the two major leagues consisted of two eight-team leagues. The 16 teams were located in ten cities, all in the northeastern and midwestern United States: New York City had three teams and <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a> each had two teams. St. Louis was the southernmost and westernmost city with a major league team. The longest possible road trip, from Boston to St. Louis, took about 24 hours by railroad. In 1953, the NL\'s Boston Braves became the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Braves" class="mw-redirect" title="Milwaukee Braves">Milwaukee Braves</a>. In 1954, the St. Louis Browns became the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Baltimore Orioles</a>. In 1955, the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Philadelphia_Athletics" title="History of the Philadelphia Athletics">Philadelphia Athletics</a> became the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Athletics" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansas City Athletics">Kansas City Athletics</a>.\n</p><p>Baseball experts consider the Brooklyn/Los Angeles Dodgers\' boss <a href="/wiki/Walter_O%27Malley" title="Walter O&#39;Malley">Walter O\'Malley</a> to be "perhaps the most influential owner of baseball\'s early expansion era."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Before the <a href="/wiki/1958_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1958 Major League Baseball season">1958 Major League Baseball season</a>, he moved the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Brooklyn_Dodgers" title="History of the Brooklyn Dodgers">Brooklyn Dodgers</a> to Los Angeles.<sup id="cite_ref-FYOL_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FYOL-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> When O\'Malley moved the Dodgers from Brooklyn, he appeared on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> O\'Malley was also influential in persuading the rival <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_New_York_Giants_(NL)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the New York Giants (NL)">New York Giants</a> to move west to become the San Francisco Giants. The Giants were already suffering from slumping attendance records at their aging ballpark, the <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a>. Had the Dodgers moved out west alone, the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a>\xe2\x80\x941,600&#160;mi (2,575&#160;km) away<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94would have been the closest NL team. The joint move made West Coast road trips economical for visiting teams.<sup id="cite_ref-WiW_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WiW-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> O\'Malley invited <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Mayor" class="mw-redirect" title="San Francisco Mayor">San Francisco Mayor</a> <a href="/wiki/George_Christopher" title="George Christopher">George Christopher</a> to New York to meet with Giants owner <a href="/wiki/Horace_Stoneham" title="Horace Stoneham">Horace Stoneham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WiW_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WiW-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Stoneham was considering moving the Giants to <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> but he was convinced to join O\'Malley on the West Coast at the end of 1957. The meetings between Stoneham, Christopher and O\'Malley occurred against the wishes of Ford Frick, the Commissioner of Baseball.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The dual moves were successful for both franchises\xe2\x80\x94and for MLB.<sup id="cite_ref-FYOL_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FYOL-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The Dodgers set a single-game MLB attendance record in their first home appearance with 78,672&#160;fans.<sup id="cite_ref-WiW_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WiW-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1961, the first <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Washington_Senators" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Washington Senators">Washington Senators</a> franchise moved to <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis_%E2%80%93_Saint_Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Minneapolis \xe2\x80\x93 Saint Paul">Minneapolis\xe2\x80\x93St. Paul</a> to become the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Twins" title="Minnesota Twins">Minnesota Twins</a>. Two new teams were added to the American League at the same time: the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Angels" title="Los Angeles Angels">Los Angeles Angels</a> (who soon moved from downtown L.A. to nearby <a href="/wiki/Anaheim,_California" title="Anaheim, California">Anaheim</a>) and a new <a href="/wiki/Washington_Senators_(1961%E2%80%931971)" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Senators (1961\xe2\x80\x931971)">Washington Senators</a> franchise. The NL added the <a href="/wiki/Houston_Astros" title="Houston Astros">Houston Astros</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a> in 1962. The Astros (known as the "Colt .45s" during their first three seasons) became the first southern major league franchise since the <a href="/wiki/Louisville_Colonels" title="Louisville Colonels">Louisville Colonels</a> folded in 1899 and the first franchise to be located along the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Gulf Coast">Gulf Coast</a>. The Mets established a reputation for futility by going 40\xe2\x80\x93120 during their first season of play in the nation\'s media capital\xe2\x80\x94and by playing only a little better in subsequent campaigns\xe2\x80\x94but in their eighth season (<a href="/wiki/1969_New_York_Mets_season" title="1969 New York Mets season">1969</a>) the Mets became the first of the 1960s expansion teams to play in the postseason, culminating in a World Series title over the heavily favored Baltimore Orioles.\n</p><p>In 1966, the major leagues moved to the "Deep South" when the Braves moved to Atlanta. In 1968, the Kansas City Athletics moved west to become the <a href="/wiki/Oakland_Athletics" title="Oakland Athletics">Oakland Athletics</a>. In 1969, the American and National Leagues both <a href="/wiki/1969_Major_League_Baseball_expansion" title="1969 Major League Baseball expansion">added two expansion franchises</a>. The American League added the <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Pilots" title="Seattle Pilots">Seattle Pilots</a> (who became the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Brewers" title="Milwaukee Brewers">Milwaukee Brewers</a> after one disastrous season in Seattle) and the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Royals" title="Kansas City Royals">Kansas City Royals</a>. The NL added the first Canadian franchise, the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Expos" title="Montreal Expos">Montreal Expos</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Padres" title="San Diego Padres">San Diego Padres</a>.\n</p><p>In 1972, the second Washington Senators moved to the <a href="/wiki/Dallas%E2%80%93Fort_Worth_metroplex" title="Dallas\xe2\x80\x93Fort Worth metroplex">Dallas\xe2\x80\x93Fort Worth metroplex</a> to become the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a>. In 1977, baseball <a href="/wiki/1977_Major_League_Baseball_expansion" title="1977 Major League Baseball expansion">expanded again</a>, adding a second Canadian team, the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Blue_Jays" title="Toronto Blue Jays">Toronto Blue Jays</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Mariners" title="Seattle Mariners">Seattle Mariners</a>. Subsequently, no new teams were added until the 1990s and no teams moved until 2005. In 1993, the NL added the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Marlins" class="mw-redirect" title="Florida Marlins">Florida Marlins</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Miami_metropolitan_area" title="Miami metropolitan area">Miami area</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Rockies" title="Colorado Rockies">Colorado Rockies</a> in Denver. In 1998, the Brewers switched leagues by joining the National League and two new teams were added: the NL\'s <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Diamondbacks" title="Arizona Diamondbacks">Arizona Diamondbacks</a> in Phoenix and the American League\'s <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Devil_Rays" class="mw-redirect" title="Tampa Bay Devil Rays">Tampa Bay Devil Rays</a> in <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg,_Florida" title="St. Petersburg, Florida">St. Petersburg, Florida</a>.\n</p><p>After the 2001 season, the team owners voted in favor of contraction. Several MLB teams had been considered for elimination in early talks about contraction, but the Montreal Expos and the Minnesota Twins were the two teams that came closest to folding under the plan. Plans for MLB contraction were halted when the Twins landlord was awarded a court injunction that required the team to play its 2002 home games at their stadium. MLB owners agreed to hold off on reducing the league\'s size until at least 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Plans_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plans-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Montreal Expos became the first franchise in over three decades to move when they became the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Nationals" title="Washington Nationals">Washington Nationals</a> in 2005. This move left Canada with just one team, but it also returned baseball to the United States capital city after a 33-year absence. This franchise shift, like many previous ones, involved baseball\'s return to a city which had been previously abandoned. Not counting the short-lived Federal League, Montreal is the only city granted an MLB franchise since 1901 that does not currently host a team.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pitching_dominance_and_rule_changes">Pitching dominance and rule changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pitching dominance and rule changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MLB_runs.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/MLB_runs.png/300px-MLB_runs.png" width="300" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/MLB_runs.png/450px-MLB_runs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/MLB_runs.png/600px-MLB_runs.png 2x" data-file-width="746" data-file-height="409" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MLB_runs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graph showing, by year, the average number of runs per MLB game</div></div></div>\n<p>By the late 1960s, the balance between pitching and hitting had swung in favor of the pitchers. In 1968\xe2\x80\x94later nicknamed "the year of the pitcher"<sup id="cite_ref-yearofthepitcher_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yearofthepitcher-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94Boston Red Sox player <a href="/wiki/Carl_Yastrzemski" title="Carl Yastrzemski">Carl Yastrzemski</a> won the American League batting title with an average of just .301, the lowest in the history of Major League Baseball.<sup id="cite_ref-yaz_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yaz-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a> pitcher <a href="/wiki/Denny_McLain" title="Denny McLain">Denny McLain</a> won 31 games, making him the only pitcher to win 30 games in a season since <a href="/wiki/Dizzy_Dean" title="Dizzy Dean">Dizzy Dean</a> in 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-denny_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-denny-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> St. Louis Cardinals starting pitcher <a href="/wiki/Bob_Gibson" title="Bob Gibson">Bob Gibson</a> achieved an equally remarkable feat by allowing an <a href="/wiki/Earned_run_average" title="Earned run average">ERA</a> of just 1.12.<sup id="cite_ref-gibson_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gibson-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following these pitching performances, in December 1968 the MLB Playing Rules Committee voted to reduce the strike zone from knees to shoulders to top of knees to armpits and lower the pitcher\'s mound from 15 to 10&#160;inches, beginning in the 1969 season.<sup id="cite_ref-mound_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mound-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1973, the American League, which had been suffering from much lower attendance than the National League, sought to increase scoring even further by initiating the designated hitter (DH) rule.<sup id="cite_ref-dh_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dh-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_stadiums_and_artificial_surfaces">New stadiums and artificial surfaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: New stadiums and artificial surfaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Throughout the 1960s and 1970s, as baseball expanded, <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">NFL</a> football had been surging in popularity, making it economical for many of these cities to build <a href="/wiki/Multi-purpose_stadium" title="Multi-purpose stadium">multi-purpose stadiums</a> instead of single-purpose baseball fields. Because of climate and economic issues, many of these facilities had playing surfaces made from <a href="/wiki/Artificial_turf" title="Artificial turf">artificial turf</a>, as well as the oval designs characteristic of stadiums designed to house both baseball and football.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> This often resulted in baseball fields with relatively more <a href="/wiki/Foul_territory" class="mw-redirect" title="Foul territory">foul territory</a> than older stadiums. These characteristics changed the nature of professional baseball, putting a higher premium on speed and defense over home-run hitting power, since the fields were often too big for teams to expect to hit many home runs and foul balls hit in the air could more easily be caught for outs.\n</p><p>Teams began to be built around pitching\xe2\x80\x94particularly their bullpens\xe2\x80\x94and speed on the basepaths. Artificial surfaces meant balls traveled quicker and bounced higher, so it became easier to hit <a href="/wiki/Ground_ball" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground ball">ground balls</a> "<a href="/wiki/In_the_hole" class="mw-redirect" title="In the hole">in the hole</a>" between the corner and middle infielders. Starting pitchers were no longer expected to throw <a href="/wiki/Complete_game" title="Complete game">complete games</a>; it was enough for a starter to pitch 6\xe2\x80\x937 innings and turn the game over to the team\'s <a href="/wiki/Closer_(baseball)" title="Closer (baseball)">closer</a>, a position which grew in importance over these decades. As stolen bases increased, home run totals dropped. After <a href="/wiki/Willie_Mays" title="Willie Mays">Willie Mays</a> hit 52 home runs in 1965, only one player (<a href="/wiki/George_Foster_(baseball)" title="George Foster (baseball)">George Foster</a>) reached that mark until the 1990s.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steroid_era">Steroid era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Steroid era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Doping_in_baseball" title="Doping in baseball">Doping in baseball</a></div>\n<p>Routinely in the late 1990s and early 2000s, baseball players hit 40 or 50 home runs in a season, a feat that was considered rare even in the 1980s. It has since become apparent that at least some of this power surge was a result of players using steroids and other performance-enhancing drugs.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uniforms">Uniforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Uniforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_uniforms" title="Major League Baseball uniforms">Major League Baseball uniforms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baseball_uniform" title="Baseball uniform">Baseball uniform</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baseball1870s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Baseball1870s.jpg/220px-Baseball1870s.jpg" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Baseball1870s.jpg/330px-Baseball1870s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/Baseball1870s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="363" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baseball1870s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A baseball team and its uniforms in the 1870s. Note that the team is integrated, in contrast to 20th century MLB, which was segregated until 1947.</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Baseball_uniform" title="Baseball uniform">baseball uniform</a> is a type of uniform worn by <a href="/wiki/Position_player" title="Position player">baseball players</a>, and by some non-playing personnel, such as <a href="/wiki/Manager_(baseball)" title="Manager (baseball)">field managers</a> and coaches. It is worn to indicate the person\'s role in the game and\xe2\x80\x94through the use of logos, colors, and <a href="/wiki/Uniform_number_(Major_League_Baseball)" title="Uniform number (Major League Baseball)">numbers</a>\xe2\x80\x94to identify the teams and their players, managers, and coaches.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Traditionally, home uniforms display the team name on the front, while away uniforms display the team\'s home location. In modern times, however, exceptions to this pattern have become common, with teams using their team name on both uniforms.<sup id="cite_ref-MLB_Uniforms_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLB_Uniforms-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Most teams also have one or more <a href="/wiki/Third_jersey" title="Third jersey">alternate uniforms</a>, usually consisting of the primary or secondary team color on the vest instead of the usual white or gray.<sup id="cite_ref-MLB_Uniforms_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLB_Uniforms-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In the past few decades <a href="/wiki/Throwback_uniform" title="Throwback uniform">throwback uniforms</a> have become popular.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The New York Knickerbockers were the first baseball team to use uniforms, taking the field on April 4, 1849, in pants made of blue wool, white <a href="/wiki/Flannel" title="Flannel">flannel</a> shirts (<a href="/wiki/Jersey_(sport)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jersey (sport)">jerseys</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Straw_hat" title="Straw hat">straw hats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baseball_cap" title="Baseball cap">Caps</a> and other types of headgear have been a part of baseball uniforms from the beginning.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Baseball teams often wore full-brimmed straw hats or no cap at all since there was no official rule regarding headgear.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Under the 1882 uniform rules, players on the same team wore uniforms of different colors and patterns that indicated which position they played.<sup id="cite_ref-The_history_of_the_baseball_uniform_at_the_National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_history_of_the_baseball_uniform_at_the_National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 1880s, the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Wolverines" title="Detroit Wolverines">Detroit Wolverines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_Nationals_(1886%E2%80%9389)" title="Washington Nationals (1886\xe2\x80\x9389)">Washington Nationals</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Bridegrooms" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooklyn Bridegrooms">Brooklyn Bridegrooms</a> of the American Association were the first to wear striped uniforms.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the 19th century, teams began the practice of having two different uniforms, one for when they played at home in their own <a href="/wiki/Baseball_park" title="Baseball park">baseball stadium</a> and a different one for when they played away (<a href="/wiki/Road_game" class="mw-redirect" title="Road game">on the road</a>) at the other team\'s ballpark.<sup id="cite_ref-MLB_Uniforms_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLB_Uniforms-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> It became common to wear white pants with a white color vest at home and gray pants with a gray or solid (dark) colored vest when away.<sup id="cite_ref-MLB_Uniforms_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLB_Uniforms-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> By 1900, both home and away uniforms were standard across the major leagues.<sup id="cite_ref-BAuni_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAuni-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Season_structure">Season structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Season structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_schedule" title="Major League Baseball schedule">Major League Baseball schedule</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spring_training">Spring training</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Spring training">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spring_training" title="Spring training">Spring training</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spring_training.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Spring_training.jpg/220px-Spring_training.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Spring_training.jpg/330px-Spring_training.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Spring_training.jpg/440px-Spring_training.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4055" data-file-height="2916" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spring_training.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Grapefruit League game at the former <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> camp in <a href="/wiki/Vero_Beach,_Florida" title="Vero Beach, Florida">Vero Beach</a>, Florida</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Spring_training" title="Spring training">Spring training</a> is a series of practices and exhibition games preceding the start of the <a href="/wiki/Regular_season" class="mw-redirect" title="Regular season">regular season</a>. Spring training allows new players to audition for roster and position spots, and gives existing team players practice time prior to competitive play. The teams are divided into the <a href="/wiki/Cactus_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Cactus League">Cactus League</a><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Grapefruit_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Grapefruit League">Grapefruit League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Spring training has always attracted fan attention, drawing crowds who travel to the warmer climates to enjoy the weather and watch their favorite teams play, and spring training usually coincides with <a href="/wiki/Spring_break" title="Spring break">spring break</a> for many college students. Autograph seekers also find greater access to players during spring training.\n</p><p>Spring training typically lasts almost two months, starting in mid February and running until just before the season opening day, traditionally the first week of April. As pitchers benefit from a longer training period, pitchers and catchers begin spring training several days before the rest of the team.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regular_season">Regular season</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Regular season">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The current MLB regular season, consisting of 162 games per team, typically begins on the first Sunday in April and ends on the first Sunday in October. Each team\'s schedule is typically organized into three-game series, with occasional two- or four-game series.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Postponed games or continuations of suspended games can result in an ad hoc one-game or five-game series. A team\'s series are organized into homestands and road trips that group multiple series together. Teams generally play games five to seven days per week, commonly having Monday or Thursday as an off day. Frequently, games are scheduled at <a href="/wiki/Night_game#Baseball" title="Night game">night</a>. Sunday games are generally played during the afternoon, allowing teams to travel to their next destination prior to a Monday night game. In addition, teams will play day games frequently on Opening Day, holidays, and getaway days.\n</p><p>Each team plays 19 games against each of its four divisional opponents. It plays one home series and one away series, amounting to six or seven games, against the 10 other teams in its league. A team also plays one of the divisions in the other league, rotating each year, with two opponents in a three-game home series, two in a three-game away series, and one with four games split between home and away. Furthermore, each team has an interleague "natural rival" (in many cases its counterpart in the same metro area) with which it plays two home games and two away games each year.\n</p><p>With an odd number of teams in each league (15), it is necessary to have two teams participate in interleague play for most days in the season, except when two or more teams have a day off. Each team plays 20 interleague games throughout the season, usually with just one interleague game per day, but for one weekend in late May all teams will participate in an interleague series. Use of the DH rule is determined by the <a href="/wiki/Home_advantage" title="Home advantage">home team\'s</a> league rules. Before 2013, interleague play was structured differently: there would be one weekend in mid-May and another period consisting typically of the last two-thirds of June in which all teams played interleague games (save for two NL teams each day), and no interleague games were scheduled outside those dates. (Before 2013, season-long interleague play was not necessary, because each league had an even number of teams. In 2013, the Houston Astros moved to the American League, so that each league would have 15 teams.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>)\n</p><p>Over the course of a season, teams compete for one of the five <a href="/wiki/Playoffs" title="Playoffs">playoff</a> berths in their league. They can win one of these berths by either winning their <a href="/wiki/MLB#League_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB">division</a>, or by capturing a <a href="/wiki/MLB_Wild_Card" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Wild Card">wild card spot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the conclusion of the 162-game season, an additional <a href="/wiki/One-game_playoff" title="One-game playoff">tie-breaking</a> game (or games) may be needed to determine <a href="/wiki/MLB_postseason" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB postseason">postseason</a> participation.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="All-Star_Game">All-Star Game</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: All-Star Game">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="Major League Baseball All-Star Game">Major League Baseball All-Star Game</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_attends_the_32nd_All-Star_Baseball_Game,_throws_out_first_ball._Speaker_of_the_House_John_W._McCormack..._-_NARA_-_194250.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/President_attends_the_32nd_All-Star_Baseball_Game%2C_throws_out_first_ball._Speaker_of_the_House_John_W._McCormack..._-_NARA_-_194250.jpg/220px-President_attends_the_32nd_All-Star_Baseball_Game%2C_throws_out_first_ball._Speaker_of_the_House_John_W._McCormack..._-_NARA_-_194250.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/President_attends_the_32nd_All-Star_Baseball_Game%2C_throws_out_first_ball._Speaker_of_the_House_John_W._McCormack..._-_NARA_-_194250.jpg/330px-President_attends_the_32nd_All-Star_Baseball_Game%2C_throws_out_first_ball._Speaker_of_the_House_John_W._McCormack..._-_NARA_-_194250.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/President_attends_the_32nd_All-Star_Baseball_Game%2C_throws_out_first_ball._Speaker_of_the_House_John_W._McCormack..._-_NARA_-_194250.jpg/440px-President_attends_the_32nd_All-Star_Baseball_Game%2C_throws_out_first_ball._Speaker_of_the_House_John_W._McCormack..._-_NARA_-_194250.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2026" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_attends_the_32nd_All-Star_Baseball_Game,_throws_out_first_ball._Speaker_of_the_House_John_W._McCormack..._-_NARA_-_194250.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> throwing out the first pitch at the 1962 All-Star Game at <a href="/wiki/RFK_Stadium" class="mw-redirect" title="RFK Stadium">DC Stadium</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In early-to-mid July, just after the midway point of the season, the Major League Baseball All-Star Game is held during a four-day break from the regular-season schedule. The All-Star Game features a team of players from the American League (AL)\xe2\x80\x94led by the manager of the previous AL World Series team\xe2\x80\x94and a team of players from the National League (NL), similarly managed, in an exhibition game. From 1959 to 1962, two games were held each season, one was held in July and one was held in August. The designated-hitter rule was used in the All-Star Game for the first time in 1989. Following games used a DH when the game was played in an AL ballpark. Since 2010, the DH rule has been in effect regardless of venue.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first official All-Star Game was held as part of the <a href="/wiki/Century_of_Progress" title="Century of Progress">1933 World\'s Fair</a> in Chicago, Illinois, and was the idea of <a href="/wiki/Arch_Ward" title="Arch Ward">Arch Ward</a>, then sports editor for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chicago_Tribune" class="mw-redirect" title="The Chicago Tribune">The Chicago Tribune</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Initially intended to be a one-time event, its great success resulted in making the game an annual one. Ward\'s contribution was recognized by Major League Baseball in 1962 with the creation of the "Arch Ward Trophy", given to the All-Star Game\'s Most Valuable Player each year.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> (In 1970, it was renamed the Commissioner\'s Trophy, until 1985, when the name change was reversed. In 2002, it was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball All-Star Game Most Valuable Player Award">Ted Williams Most Valuable Player Award</a>.)\n</p><p>Beginning in 1947, the eight position players in each team\'s starting lineup have been voted into the game by fans.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The fan voting was discontinued after a 1957 ballot-box-stuffing scandal in Cincinnati: seven of the eight slots originally went to <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Reds" title="Cincinnati Reds">Reds</a> players, two of whom were subsequently removed from the lineup to make room for Willie Mays and <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a>. Fan voting was reinstated in 1970 and has continued ever since, including Internet voting in recent years.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/2002_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="2002 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">2002 contest in Milwaukee</a> controversially ended in an 11-inning tie when both managers ran out of pitchers. In response, starting in 2003 the league which wins the All-Star game received home-field advantage in the World Series: the league champion hosted the first two games at its own ballpark as well as the last two (if necessary). The National League did not win an All-Star game and thus gain home-field advantage until 2010; it was able to overcome this disadvantage and win in three of the seven World Series from 2003 to 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> This was discontinued after the 2016 season. \n</p><p>MLB All-Stars from both leagues have worn uniforms from their respective teams at the game with one exception. In the <a href="/wiki/1933_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="1933 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">1933 All-Star Game</a>, the National League All-Star Team members wore special gray uniforms with "National League" written in navy blue letters across the front of the jersey.<sup id="cite_ref-The_World_Series_and_Highlights_of_Baseball_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_Series_and_Highlights_of_Baseball-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baseball_uniforms_of_the_20th_century_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baseball_uniforms_of_the_20th_century-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postseason">Postseason</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_League_Baseball&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Postseason">edit</a><span
